## YOUR INPUTS

### INTRODUCTION ESSAY (for review)
# Psalm 145: The Democracy of Divine Praise

Psalm 145 stands alone in the Psalter. It is the only psalm titled *tehillah* (תְּהִלָּה, "a song of praise") rather than the standard *mizmor* (מִזְמוֹר, "a psalm"), marking it as something special from the outset. This distinction signals more than editorial preference; it announces a psalm that attempts nothing less than to orchestrate universal praise of Israel's God. Through its carefully constructed acrostic framework and expanding rhetorical strategy, Psalm 145 presents a revolutionary vision of divine kingship that transforms Ancient Near Eastern royal ideology by depicting YHWH as both cosmically sovereign and intimately accessible—a king whose eternal reign manifests not in distant majesty but in providential care for all creation.

The psalm's architecture reveals its theological agenda. Moving through the Hebrew alphabet from *aleph* to *tav* (with one conspicuous gap), the poem creates what we might call concentric circles of praise that expand outward from the personal to the universal. The psalmist begins with intimate commitment: "I will extol you, my God and king" (אֲרוֹמִמְךָ֣ אֱלוֹהַ֣י הַמֶּ֑לֶךְ, v. 1). The juxtaposition is striking—*elohai hamelekh* combines the most intimate divine designation ("my God") with the most public ("the king"). This tension between intimacy and majesty drives the entire composition.

From this personal starting point, the psalm systematically expands its vision. Verses 4-7 invoke "generation to generation" (*dor ledor*, דּ֣וֹר לְ֭דוֹר) as the mechanism for transmitting divine praise across time. The language here is pedagogical: generations will "declare" (*yaggidu*, יַגִּידוּ) and "utter" (*yabbiʿu*, יַבִּ֑יעוּ) God's works. The verb *yabbiʿu*, typically used for water gushing from a spring, transforms speech into a natural force—praise becomes as inevitable and abundant as flowing water.

The psalm's theological center arrives in verses 8-9 with the citation of the classic covenant formula from Exodus 34:6: "gracious and merciful is the LORD, slow to anger and great in kindness" (חַנּוּן֙ וְרַח֣וּם ה׳, אֶ֣רֶךְ אַפַּ֔יִם וּגְדׇל־חָֽסֶד). But here the psalmist performs a radical expansion. Where Exodus applies these attributes to Israel specifically, Psalm 145 universalizes them: "The LORD is good to all, and His mercy is upon all His works" (טוֹב־ה׳ לַכֹּ֑ל וְרַחֲמָ֖יו עַל־כׇּל־מַעֲשָֽׂיו, v. 9). The double use of *kol* ("all") is no accident—the psalmist is systematically extending covenant language to encompass all creation.

This universalization reaches its crescendo in verses 10-13 with the declaration of divine kingship using language borrowed directly from Canaanite royal ideology. The phrase "Your kingdom is a kingdom of all ages, and Your dominion is throughout all generations" (מַלְכֽוּתְךָ֗ מַלְכ֥וּת כׇּל־עֹֽלָמִ֑ים וּמֶמְשַׁלְתְּךָ֖ בְּכׇל־דּ֣וֹר וָדֹֽר, v. 13) finds a striking parallel in the Ugaritic *Baal* Cycle, where similar language celebrates divine rule. The psalmist appropriates this common Ancient Near Eastern royal vocabulary but redirects it toward Israel's God, claiming not just political supremacy but cosmic sovereignty.

Yet the psalm's most startling move comes in verse 14 with a dramatic shift from cosmic kingship to intimate caregiving: "The LORD upholds all who fall and raises up all who are bowed down" (סוֹמֵ֣ךְ ה׳ לְכׇל־הַנֹּפְלִ֑ים וְזוֹקֵ֖ף לְכׇל־הַכְּפוּפִֽים). The Hebrew participles *somekh* (סוֹמֵךְ, "supporting") and *zoqef* (זוֹקֵף, "straightening") describe God as actively engaged in lifting up the vulnerable. This is not the aloof monarch of typical royal ideology but a king whose sovereignty manifests in compassionate intervention.

The feeding imagery that follows (vv. 15-16) reinforces this inversion of royal expectations. "The eyes of all look to You expectantly, and You give them their food in its season. You open Your hand and satisfy every living thing with favor" (עֵינֵי־כֹ֭ל אֵלֶ֣יךָ יְשַׂבֵּ֑רוּ וְאַתָּ֤ה נוֹתֵ֨ן לָהֶ֖ם אֶת־אׇכְלָ֣ם בְּעִתּֽוֹ׃ פּוֹתֵ֥חַ אֶת־יָדֶ֑ךָ וּמַשְׂבִּ֖יעַ לְכׇל־חַ֣י רָצֽוֹן). The phrase "opening Your hand" (*poteaḥ et-yadekha*, פּוֹתֵ֥חַ אֶת־יָדֶ֑ךָ) transforms a simple gesture into cosmic provision. Traditional interpretation debates whether *ratzon* (רָצֽוֹן) refers to God's will or creature's satisfaction, but the context suggests both: God's will manifests precisely in satisfying every creature's desire.

The psalm's only negative note appears in verse 20 with the promise that "all the wicked He will destroy" (וְאֵ֖ת כׇּל־הָרְשָׁעִ֣ים יַשְׁמִֽיד). This might seem to contradict the universal care celebrated throughout, but rabbinic interpretation offers insight. Rashi suggests this destruction is ultimately redemptive—wickedness itself must be eliminated for universal flourishing to occur. The psalm envisions not the annihilation of persons but the eradication of evil that prevents creation from experiencing God's full provision.

One puzzle remains: the missing *nun* verse in the acrostic. While we cannot recover the original line (if one existed), its absence creates an intriguing gap in the psalm's completeness—perhaps a reminder that even our most comprehensive praise falls short of divine reality. The *nun* would have introduced a verse about falling (*naphal*, the Hebrew root beginning with *nun*), making Rashi's observation particularly apt: David omitted this letter because he "saw in it a hard fall" but then "supported it with the Holy Spirit" through verse 14's promise that "the LORD supports all who fall."

The psalm concludes with a return to personal commitment that now encompasses universal scope: "My mouth shall speak the praise of the LORD, and all flesh shall bless His holy name forever and ever" (תְּהִלַּ֤ת ה׳ ׀ יְֽדַבֶּר־פִּ֗י וִיבָרֵ֣ךְ כׇּל־בָּ֭שָׂר שֵׁ֥ם קׇדְשׁ֗וֹ לְעוֹלָ֥ם וָעֶֽד, v. 21). The phrase "all flesh" (*kol-basar*, כׇּל־בָּ֭שָׂר) extends beyond humanity to encompass all living beings. The psalmist's individual voice becomes the catalyst for cosmic chorus.

This vision of divine kingship represents a profound departure from Ancient Near Eastern royal ideology. Where typical royal theology emphasized the monarch's power to command and destroy, Psalm 145 presents a king whose greatness manifests in nurture, whose strength appears in support for the vulnerable, and whose sovereignty extends not through conquest but through provision. The result is what we might call a "democracy of divine praise"—not because God's rule is democratic, but because His care extends democratically to all creation, making every creature a potential participant in the cosmic liturgy.

The psalm's enduring power lies in this fusion of majesty and accessibility. By systematically expanding the circles of divine care from personal to generational to universal, it creates space for every reader to find themselves somewhere in the widening gyre of God's attention. In doing so, it offers a vision of divine kingship that is both utterly transcendent and surprisingly intimate—a theology that can encompass both the vastness of "all worlds" and the particular needs of individual creatures waiting for their daily bread.

### VERSE-BY-VERSE COMMENTARY (for review)
# Verse-by-Verse Commentary on Psalm 145

**Verse 1**
The unique superscription תְּהִלָּה לְדָוִד ("A song of praise. Of David") immediately signals this psalm's distinctive character—it is the only psalm in the Psalter titled תְּהִלָּה rather than the standard מִזְמוֹר. This terminological choice is theologically loaded: while מִזְמוֹר suggests instrumental accompaniment, תְּהִלָּה emphasizes the act of praise itself. The medieval commentator Radak noted that this title anticipates the psalm's structure, beginning and ending with the root הלל (praise), creating a perfect inclusio of laudation.

The opening declaration אֲרוֹמִמְךָ֣ אֱלוֹהַ֣י הַמֶּ֑לֶךְ ("I will extol You, my God and king") contains a striking grammatical tension. The intimate possessive pronoun in אֱלוֹהַי ("my God") collides with the universal title הַמֶּלֶךְ ("the king"), creating what might be called a "personal-cosmic paradox." This juxtaposition—found nowhere else in Scripture with this precise formulation—establishes the psalm's central theological agenda: bridging the gap between private devotion and cosmic sovereignty. The verb אֲרוֹמִמְךָ, from the root רום (to be high), carries connotations of both physical elevation and honor. Throughout Scripture, this root appears in contexts where divine transcendence meets human accessibility—notably in Isaiah's temple vision (6:1) and in Moses' song at the sea (Ex. 15:2).

The temporal commitment לְעוֹלָם וָעֶד ("forever and ever") appears five times in the Psalter, but only here does it frame the entire composition as an inclusio (returning in v. 21). This creates what we might call "temporal infinity brackets," suggesting that genuine praise transcends the boundaries of individual human existence. Traditional Jewish interpretation sees this as a reference to both this world and the world to come, making the psalmist's commitment not merely lifelong but eschatological.

**Verse 2**
The intensification from verse 1 continues with the temporal marker בְּכׇל־יוֹם ("every day"), which transforms praise from occasional expression to quotidian practice. This phrase appears 17 times in Scripture, typically emphasizing routine obligations (as in priestly duties in 1 Chronicles 16:37), but here it sanctifies the mundane temporality of daily life. The psalmist refuses to relegate praise to special occasions—it becomes as regular as sunrise.

The parallel verbs אֲבָרְכֶךָּ ("I will bless You") and אֲהַלְלָה ("I will praise You") create what Robert Alter calls "semantic intensification"—each verb builds upon the previous one. The root ברך (to bless) typically flows from superior to inferior (God blessing humans), but here it reverses direction, suggesting that human blessing of God involves recognizing and proclaiming His providential goodness. The verb הלל (to praise) adds the dimension of public proclamation—what begins as personal commitment expands toward communal witness.

The repetition of לְעוֹלָם וָעֶד creates an immediate echo effect, reinforcing the temporal infinitude introduced in verse 1. This doubling functions poetically as what Hebrew rhetoric calls "repetitive parallelism," but theologically it suggests that both blessing and praise transcend temporal limitations. The medieval commentator Ibn Ezra noted that the repetition emphasizes the dual nature of human response to divine kingship—both gratitude (blessing) and proclamation (praise).

**Verse 3**
The declaration of divine incomprehensibility גָּד֣וֹל יְהוָה֘ וּמְהֻלָּ֪ל מְ֫אֹ֥ד ("Great is the LORD and greatly to be praised") employs a figura etymologica with the root גדל, creating sonic emphasis on divine magnitude. The phrase וְלִגְדֻלָּתוֹ אֵ֣ין חֵֽקֶר ("His greatness cannot be fathomed") introduces the theme of divine incomprehensibility using the root חקר, which appears 25 times in Scripture typically in contexts of searching or investigating (as in Job's question "Can you fathom God?" 11:7).

This verse functions as the theological hinge between personal commitment (vv. 1-2) and generational transmission (vv. 4-7). The movement from "I will extol" to "His greatness cannot be fathomed" acknowledges a fundamental limitation: individual praise, however fervent, cannot exhaust divine reality. The noun גְדֻלָּה (greatness) appears 23 times in Scripture, often in contexts emphasizing divine acts in history (Deut. 3:24; 5:24), but here it suggests ontological magnitude beyond human comprehension.

The phrase אֵין חֵקֶר ("cannot be fathomed") uses the metaphor of depth-sounding, as though divine greatness were an ocean whose bottom cannot be reached. This imagery appears in Job 5:9 ("does great things beyond understanding") and Isaiah 40:28 ("His understanding cannot be fathomed"), establishing a canonical tradition of acknowledging divine incomprehensibility as a reason for, rather than obstacle to, praise. The medieval commentator Metzudat David noted that this verse transitions from what humans can do (praise daily) to what only God can be (infinitely great), preparing for the communal expansion that follows.

**Verse 4**
The phrase דּ֣וֹר לְ֭דוֹר ("generation to generation") introduces the psalm's first major expansion, moving from individual praise to intergenerational transmission. This construction appears only twice in Scripture (here and Isaiah 34:10), making it a rare and therefore emphatic formulation. The verb יְשַׁבַּח (from שבח, "to praise") occurs 11 times in the Hebrew Bible, typically in contexts of public proclamation, while יַגִּידוּ (from נגד, "to declare") appears over 300 times, often in contexts of bearing witness or testimony.

The objects of this transmission—מַעֲשֶׂיךָ ("Your works") and גְבוּרֹתֶיךָ ("Your mighty acts")—represent two different categories of divine action. The term מַעֲשֶׂה typically refers to God's creative and providential activity (as in Genesis 2:2-3), while גְבוּרָה emphasizes divine power displayed in historical intervention (as in Exodus 15:6; Psalm 106:2). This pairing suggests that each generation must transmit both the regular evidences of divine providence and the extraordinary demonstrations of divine power.

The syntax creates what we might call "testimonial momentum"—one generation will praise, and as a consequence, they will declare mighty acts to the next. This establishes praise not merely as personal expression but as pedagogical responsibility. The medieval commentator Radak noted that this verse explains why individual praise (vv. 1-3) is insufficient: divine greatness demands communal and historical witness. The Targum renders יְשַׁבַּח as "will give thanks," emphasizing the gratitude dimension of intergenerational praise transmission.

**Verse 5**
The phrase הֲדַר כְּבוֹד הוֹדֶךָ creates an extraordinary accumulation of three glory-words in Hebrew: הֲדַר (majesty/splendor), כְּבוֹד (glory/weight), and הוֹד (majesty/vigor). This triple heaping appears nowhere else in Scripture with this exact combination, creating what we might call a "glory cascade." Each term contributes distinct semantic nuances: הֲדַר suggests visual splendor, כְּבוֹד implies substantial weight or importance, and הוֹד conveys vigorous majesty. The construct chain arrangement intensifies the effect—not merely three separate attributes but an ascending spiral of divine magnificence.

The syntax shifts dramatically from third person (talking about God to others) back to first person singular: אָשִׂיחָה ("I will meditate"). The verb שיח typically means "to muse" or "to meditate" rather than simply "to speak," appearing 20 times in Scripture in contexts of contemplative reflection (notably Psalm 119:15, 23, 48). This creates an interesting theological psychology: after speaking of generational transmission, the psalmist returns to personal contemplation, suggesting that public proclamation must be grounded in private meditation.

The object of this meditation—נִפְלְאֹתֶיךָ ("Your wondrous deeds")—uses a term that appears 71 times in Scripture, typically for acts that exceed normal causation and reveal divine intervention. The root פלא suggests "distinction" or "separation" from ordinary experience. Ibn Ezra noted that this word choice indicates not merely great deeds but acts that demonstrate God's transcendence of natural limitations. The return to first person creates what might be called a "contemplative pause" in the psalm's expanding circles of praise—individual reflection anchors communal proclamation.

**Verse 6**
This verse presents a textual puzzle with the qere/ketiv variant involving וּגְדֻלָּתְךָ/וְגַדְלוּתֶ֖יךָ ("Your greatness"), indicating ancient scribal uncertainty about the precise form. The phrase עֱזוּז נֽוֹרְאֹתֶ֣יךָ ("the power of Your awesome deeds") combines two potent terms: עֱזוּז (fierce strength) and נוֹרָאוֹת (fearful/awesome acts). The word עֱזוּז appears only here in Scripture, making it a hapax legomenon that intensifies the uniqueness of divine power. It derives from עזז (to be strong), but its form suggests concentrated, almost fierce strength.

The verb יֹאמֵרוּ ("they shall speak") shifts to third person plural, while אֲסַפְּרֶנָּה ("I will declare it") returns to first person, creating a dialogue effect between communal proclamation and personal testimony. This alternation appears nowhere else in the Psalter with this specific pattern, suggesting a carefully crafted interplay between collective and individual witness. The verb ספר (to recount/number) often implies detailed enumeration—not merely mentioning but carefully cataloguing divine acts.

The medieval commentator Metzudat David noted that this verse emphasizes the overwhelming nature of divine acts: others speak of their power, but the psalmist commits to declaring their greatness in detail. The phrase creates what we might call "testimonial complementarity"—the community acknowledges divine power, while the individual takes responsibility for detailed recounting. This division of labor in praise suggests that comprehensive response to divine greatness requires both collective recognition and individual commitment to detailed witness.

**Verse 7**
The phrase זֵכֶר רַב־טוּבְךָ ("the remembrance of Your abundant goodness") introduces the important concept of זֵכֶר (memorial/remembrance), which appears 23 times in Scripture typically referring to something preserved for future commemoration. The term רַב־טוּב (abundant goodness) creates an unusual compound emphasizing both quantity and quality of divine beneficence. The construct relationship suggests not merely "good things" but "goodness itself in abundance."

The verb יַבִּיעוּ ("they shall gush forth") provides one of Scripture's most vivid metaphors for speech. From the root נבע (to bubble up/flow), it appears 11 times in biblical literature, typically describing liquid emerging from a spring (Isaiah 59:7; Proverbs 18:4). When applied to speech, it transforms human testimony into a natural force—praise becomes as inevitable and abundant as water flowing from an artesian well. This same verb appears in Psalm 78:2 ("I will pour forth dark sayings") and 119:171 ("My lips shall pour forth praise"), establishing a pattern of speech as overflowing liquid.

The parallel verb יְרַנֵּנוּ ("they shall sing joyfully") from רנן (to cry out/sing) appears 54 times in Scripture, often in contexts of communal celebration. The pairing of "gushing forth remembrance" with "singing righteousness" creates what might be called "testimonial abundance"—human response to divine goodness flows like water and soars like song. Traditional interpretation (Radak) sees this as describing the messianic age when knowledge of God's goodness will be so overwhelming that praise becomes as natural and abundant as springs of water. The verse suggests that adequate response to divine goodness requires both cognitive remembrance (זֵכֶר) and emotional expression (רנן).

**Verse 8**
This verse quotes the foundational covenant formula from Exodus 34:6: חַנּוּן וְרַחוּם יְהוָה אֶרֶךְ אַפַּיִם וּגְדׇל־חָסֶד ("The LORD is gracious and merciful, slow to anger and great in kindness"). This formulaic language appears in variant forms throughout Scripture (Numbers 14:18; Nehemiah 9:17; Psalms 86:15; 103:8; Joel 2:13; Jonah 4:2), making it one of the most-cited divine attribute lists in the Hebrew Bible. However, Psalm 145 makes a crucial theological move: where Exodus 34:6 applies these attributes specifically to Israel in the context of covenant renewal after the golden calf incident, here they become the foundation for universal divine care.

The four attributes create a chiastic structure: gracious (חַנּוּן) and merciful (רַחוּם) frame the central pair of slow to anger (אֶרֶךְ אַפַּיִם) and great in kindness (גְדׇל־חָסֶד). The term חַנּוּן (gracious) from חנן appears 77 times in Scripture, typically describing unmerited favor. The word רַחוּם (merciful) from רחם suggests the compassion of a parent for a child, appearing 13 times in divine descriptions. The phrase אֶרֶךְ אַפַּיִם literally means "long of nostrils," using the metaphor of delayed breathing to describe patience—divine anger is "slow to heat."

The attribute גְדׇל־חָסֶד ("great in kindness") uses the covenant term חֶסֶד, which appears 245 times in Scripture and encompasses loyalty, faithfulness, and steadfast love. Ibn Ezra noted that these four attributes cover the complete spectrum of divine relationship: grace (unmerited favor), mercy (compassion for weakness), patience (restraint of anger), and faithfulness (reliable commitment). The citation of this classical formula at the psalm's theological center establishes covenant faithfulness as the foundation for the universal care celebrated in the following verse.

**Verse 9**
The revolutionary expansion טוֹב־יְהוָה לַכֹּ֑ל וְרַחֲמָ֖יו עַל־כׇּל־מַעֲשָֽׂיו ("The LORD is good to all, and His mercy is upon all His works") represents one of Scripture's most audacious theological claims. The double use of כֹּל ("all") is emphatic and unprecedented in this formulation. While the covenant attributes of verse 8 traditionally apply to Israel, verse 9 universalizes them to encompass כׇּל־מַעֲשָֽׂיו ("all His works")—a phrase that includes all creation, not merely humanity.

The phrase וְרַחֲמָ֖יו עַל־כׇּל־מַעֲשָֽׂיו uses the spatial metaphor of mercy "upon" or "over" (עַל) creation, suggesting divine compassion as a covering or atmosphere that envelops all existence. The word רַחֲמָיו (His mercies) is plural, possibly suggesting multiple manifestations or inexhaustible dimensions of divine compassion. This represents a stunning theological development: the intensive divine attributes revealed in covenant relationship now extend to cosmic scope.

Radak noted that this verse explains why humans should emulate divine compassion toward all creatures, not merely other humans. The universalization creates what we might call "cosmic covenant"—the same relational qualities that bind God to Israel now characterize God's relationship to all creation. This prepares for verses 10-13, where "all works" join "faithful ones" in a chorus of praise. The medieval commentator Meiri observed that this verse provides theological grounding for ethical treatment of animals and care for creation, since all participate in divine mercy. The verse transforms covenant theology into creation theology, making particularistic divine attributes the foundation for universal divine care.

**Verse 10**
The phrase יוֹד֣וּךָ יְ֭הוָה כׇּל־מַעֲשֶׂ֑יךָ ("All Your works shall give You thanks, O LORD") makes an extraordinary claim: creation itself becomes a conscious participant in praise. The verb ידה (to give thanks/confess) appears 114 times in Scripture, typically describing human acknowledgment of divine goodness, but here it's attributed to כׇּל־מַעֲשֶׂיךָ ("all Your works"). This personification of creation appears elsewhere in Scripture (Psalm 98:8, "let the rivers clap their hands"; Isaiah 55:12, "the trees of the field shall clap their hands"), but nowhere else with this comprehensive scope.

The parallel וַחֲסִידֶ֖יךָ יְבָרְכֽוּכָה ("and Your faithful ones shall bless You") creates a two-choir structure: creation offering thanks (יוֹדוּךָ) and the faithful offering blessing (יְבָרְכֽוּכָה). The term חֲסִידִים (faithful/pious ones) appears 25 times in the Psalter, typically referring to those who practice covenant faithfulness. The verb יְבָרְכֽוּכָה (they shall bless You) reverses the typical direction of blessing, as humans bless God rather than receiving blessing from God.

This verse establishes what we might call "liturgical democracy"—both creation and covenant community participate in praise, though with different modes of expression. Ibn Ezra noted that creation gives thanks through its orderly functioning and beauty, while the faithful add conscious articulation of divine goodness. The two different verbs (יוֹדוּךָ and יְבָרְכֽוּכָה) suggest distinct but complementary forms of response: creation's thanksgiving is spontaneous and natural, while the faithful's blessing is intentional and covenantal. This prepares for the focus on divine kingship in the following verses, where both created order and human loyalty acknowledge divine sovereignty.

**Verse 11**
The phrase כְּב֣וֹד מַלְכוּתְךָ֣ יֹאמֵ֑רוּ ("They shall speak of the glory of Your kingdom") introduces the psalm's central royal theology section. The term מַלְכוּת (kingdom/kingship) appears 91 times in Scripture, but its pairing with כְּבוֹד (glory/weight) creates emphasis on divine rule as inherently magnificent rather than merely powerful. The verb יֹאמֵ֑רוּ ("they shall speak") continues the third-person plural from verse 10, suggesting that both creation and the faithful participate in proclaiming divine kingship.

The parallel וּגְבוּרָתְךָ֣ יְדַבֵּֽרוּ ("and they shall talk of Your power") uses יְדַבֵּֽרוּ (they shall speak/talk), which appears over 1100 times in Scripture and suggests more detailed, discursive speech than the simple יֹאמֵ֑רוּ. The word גְבוּרָה (power/might) appears 61 times in Scripture, typically referring to divine strength demonstrated in historical acts (Exodus 15:6; Psalm 106:2). The pairing of מַלְכוּת and גְבוּרָה suggests that divine kingship manifests through demonstrated power rather than mere claims to authority.

The repetitive parallelism (they shall speak...they shall speak) creates what Hebrew poetics calls "incremental repetition," where each line builds upon the previous one. This verse functions as the announcement that prepares for verse 12's explanation of purpose: this proclamation of divine kingship serves to inform humanity of God's royal acts and glory. Medieval commentator Radak noted that the double emphasis on speaking suggests both spontaneous recognition and deliberate proclamation of divine rule, making every act of praise a political statement about ultimate sovereignty. The verse transforms worship into a form of royal recognition, where praise becomes acknowledgment of divine kingship.

**Verse 12**
The purpose clause לְהוֹדִ֤יעַ לִבְנֵ֨י הָאָדָ֗ם גְּבוּרֹתָ֗יו ("to make known to the sons of men His mighty deeds") provides the pedagogical rationale for the proclamation described in verse 11. The phrase בְּנֵ֨י הָאָדָ֗ם appears 11 times in Scripture (including Ecclesiastes 1:13; 2:3; 3:10, 18; 9:12), typically emphasizing universal humanity rather than specific covenant communities. This universalizing language aligns with verse 9's extension of divine mercy to "all His works"—the proclamation of divine kingship serves not merely internal community edification but global human instruction.

The parallel phrase וּכְב֥וֹד הֲדַ֖ר מַלְכוּתֽוֹ ("and the glorious majesty of His kingdom") creates another triple accumulation of royal terminology: כְבוֹד (glory), הֲדַר (majesty), and מַלְכוּת (kingdom). This builds on verse 5's glory-cascade (הֲדַר כְּבוֹד הוֹדֶךָ) but now applies it specifically to divine kingship. The construct relationship כְב֥וֹד הֲדַ֖ר מַלְכוּתֽוֹ suggests not three separate attributes but a unified reality of magnificent royal rule.

This verse establishes what we might call "missionary monarchy"—divine kingship is not a private truth for covenant communities but public reality that must be proclaimed לִבְנֵ֨י הָאָדָ֗ם ("to human beings universally"). The medieval commentator Metzudat David noted that this creates evangelical responsibility: those who recognize divine kingship must make it known to those who do not. The verse transforms worship from private devotion or even community celebration into public testimony about ultimate political reality. The pedagogy serves apologetic purposes: demonstration of divine גְּבוּרוֹת (mighty deeds) provides evidence for claims about divine מַלְכוּת (kingship), making praise into a form of rational persuasion about cosmic governance.

**Verse 13**
The declaration מַלְכוּתְךָ֗ מַלְכ֥וּת כׇּל־עֹלָמִ֑ים וּמֶמְשַׁלְתְּךָ֖ בְּכׇל־דּ֣וֹר וָדֹֽר ("Your kingdom is a kingdom for all ages, and Your dominion endures throughout all generations") contains one of Scripture's most significant parallels to Ancient Near Eastern royal ideology. This formulation finds a striking parallel in the Ugaritic Baal Cycle (KTU 1.2 IV 10), where similar language celebrates divine rule: "Your eternal kingship, your everlasting dominion." The appropriation of this common ANE royal formula shows the psalmist claiming for YHWH the cosmic sovereignty typically attributed to various national deities.

The repetitive construction מַלְכוּת...מַלְכ֥וּת creates emphatic assertion through word repetition, while the parallel מֶמְשַׁלְתְּךָ֖ (Your dominion) adds the dimension of active governance. The phrase כׇּל־עֹלָמִ֑ים (all ages/worlds) suggests both temporal infinity and spatial universality, while בְּכׇל־דּ֣וֹר וָדֹֽר (in all generations) emphasizes continuity through human history. This creates a comprehensive claim: divine rule encompasses all time, all space, and all human generations.

The Septuagint (LXX) adds an extra line not found in the Hebrew Masoretic Text: "The Lord is faithful in all his words and holy in all his works" (πιστὸς κύριος ἐν πᾶσι τοῖς λόγοις αὐτοῦ καὶ ὅσιος ἐν πᾶσι τοῖς ἔργοις αὐτοῦ). This addition, whether reflecting a different Hebrew Vorlage or interpretive expansion, emphasizes divine reliability—eternal kingship requires faithful character. The textual variation reveals ancient interpretive concern: cosmic claims about divine rule needed grounding in experienced divine faithfulness. Ibn Ezra noted that unlike human kingdoms that end with death or conquest, divine מַלְכוּת has no temporal or spatial limitations, making YHWH the only true king in ultimate terms.

**Verse 14**
The dramatic tonal shift in סוֹמֵ֣ךְ יְ֭הוָה לְכׇל־הַנֹּפְלִ֑ים וְזוֹקֵ֖ף לְכׇל־הַכְּפוּפִֽים ("The LORD upholds all who fall and raises up all who are bowed down") represents one of Scripture's most stunning transitions from cosmic majesty to intimate care. After three verses of royal theology emphasizing universal divine kingship, the psalm suddenly pivots to focus on individual human vulnerability. This shift appears nowhere else in biblical literature with such dramatic intensity.

The participles סוֹמֵ֣ךְ (supporting/upholding) and זוֹקֵ֖ף (straightening/raising up) describe ongoing divine activity rather than past accomplishments. The verb סמך (to support) appears 48 times in Scripture, often describing physical propping up (2 Kings 18:21, a broken reed as support), but here it metaphorically describes divine assistance to הַנֹּפְלִ֑ים (those who fall/stumble). The parallel verb זקף (to raise up) appears only 4 times in Scripture, making it relatively rare and therefore emphatic.

The universal scope לְכׇל־הַנֹּפְלִ֑ים...לְכׇל־הַכְּפוּפִֽים ("all who fall...all who are bowed down") maintains the psalm's comprehensive vision while focusing on human need. The participles הַנֹּפְלִ֑ים (falling ones) and הַכְּפוּפִֽים (bowed down ones) use physical metaphors for various forms of human distress—economic, social, spiritual, or physical. Rashi's famous comment connects this verse to the missing nun letter in the acrostic: David omitted nun because he "saw in it a hard fall" (referring to Amos 5:2, "Fallen, no more to rise, is the virgin Israel"), but then "supported it with the Holy Spirit" through this promise of divine support. This verse transforms royal ideology from triumph over enemies to care for the vulnerable, making divine kingship fundamentally pastoral rather than merely political.

**Verse 15**
The phrase עֵינֵי־כֹ֭ל אֵלֶ֣יךָ יְשַׂבֵּ֑רוּ ("The eyes of all look to You expectantly") creates one of Scripture's most vivid images of universal dependence. The construct עֵינֵי־כֹ֭ל (eyes of all) personalizes creation's dependence through body metaphor—not merely "all creatures" but specifically their "eyes," emphasizing the visual dimension of hope and expectation. The verb יְשַׂבֵּ֑רוּ (they hope/wait expectantly) from שבר appears only 7 times in Scripture, making it relatively rare. This Aramaic loanword suggests patient, confident waiting rather than desperate pleading.

The response וְאַתָּ֤ה נוֹתֵ֨ן לָהֶ֖ם אֶת־אׇכְלָ֣ם בְּעִתּֽוֹ ("and You give them their food in its season") emphasizes divine reliability through the temporal phrase בְּעִתּֽוֹ (in its time/season). This phrase appears 14 times in Scripture, typically emphasizing proper timing as crucial for effectiveness (Ecclesiastes 3:1, "To everything there is a season"; Ezra 10:14, "at appointed times"). The word אֹ֫כֶל (food) rather than לֶחֶם (bread) suggests provision for all creatures, not merely humans.

This verse establishes what we might call "providential monarchy"—divine kingship manifests not through conquest or domination but through reliable provision for creaturely needs. The metaphor of eyes looking upward appears elsewhere in Scripture (Psalm 25:15, "My eyes are always toward the LORD"; 121:1, "I lift up my eyes to the hills"), but nowhere else with this comprehensive scope (כֹ֭ל, "all"). The image transforms the food chain into a prayer chain, with all creation looking expectantly toward divine provision. Medieval commentator Meiri noted that this verse explains why divine kingship differs from human kingship: human kings expect tribute from subjects, while the divine king provides sustenance to subjects. The expectant waiting described here is not passive but participatory—creation cooperates with divine timing rather than demanding immediate satisfaction.

**Verse 16**
The image פּוֹתֵ֥חַ אֶת־יָדֶ֑ךָ ("You open Your hand") provides one of Scripture's most anthropomorphic descriptions of divine provision, yet it functions as a powerful idiom for generosity throughout biblical literature. The phrase appears in various forms 4 times in Scripture (Deuteronomy 15:8, 11; Psalm 104:28; and here), always in contexts emphasizing generous giving. The simple gesture of opening one's hand becomes a metaphor for divine accessibility and abundance—what humans must deliberately choose (opening their hands to the poor), God does naturally and continuously.

The result clause וּמַשְׂבִּ֖יעַ לְכׇל־חַ֣י רָצֽוֹן ("and satisfy every living thing with favor") contains a fascinating interpretive puzzle with the word רָצֽוֹן. This term can mean either "will/desire" (suggesting God satisfies each creature's desires) or "favor/acceptance" (suggesting God's gracious will provides satisfaction). Traditional interpreters divided: Ibn Ezra understood it as "what is sufficient for them," while Radak interpreted it as "each according to its desire." The ambiguity may be deliberate, suggesting both divine grace and creaturely satisfaction converge in providential care.

The phrase לְכׇל־חַ֣י (to every living thing) extends even beyond the comprehensive כֹ֭ל (all) of verse 15 to specify כַ֣י (living beings), emphasizing the life-sustaining dimension of divine provision. This verse transforms the anthropomorphic metaphor of God's "opened hand" into what we might call "cosmic hospitality"—the divine host providing for all guests at creation's table. Medieval commentator Malbim noted that the opened hand image suggests divine provision requires no effort or reluctance, unlike human giving which often involves internal struggle about generosity. The verse presents divine providence as natural overflow rather than calculated distribution, making creation's dependence (verse 15) meet divine abundance (verse 16) in perfect harmony.

**Verse 17**
The declaration צַדִּ֣יק יְ֭הוָה בְּכׇל־דְּרָכָ֑יו וְחָסִ֖יד בְּכׇל־מַעֲשָֽׂיו ("The LORD is righteous in all His ways and faithful in all His works") applies two typically human virtues to divine character in an unusual formulation. While צַדִּיק (righteous) appears 206 times in Scripture frequently describing divine character, חָסִיד (faithful/loyal) appears only 32 times, and when applied to God (rather than describing human faithfulness) it occurs only here and in Jeremiah 3:12. This creates what we might call "moral anthropomorphism"—divine character described through human ethical categories.

The parallel structure בְּכׇל־דְּרָכָ֑יו...בְּכׇל־מַעֲשָֽׂיו ("in all His ways...in all His works") creates comprehensive scope through the double כׇל (all), while distinguishing between דְּרָכִים (ways/paths) and מַעֲשִׂים (works/deeds). The term דֶּרֶךְ (way) appears over 700 times in Scripture, often metaphorically for manner of life or conduct, while מַעֲשֶׂה (work) typically refers to specific accomplishments. The distinction suggests divine righteousness characterizes both God's general conduct (ways) and particular acts (works).

This verse functions as theological foundation for the accessibility promised in verse 18—divine righteousness and faithfulness make God reliable for those who call upon Him. The application of חָסִיד to God is particularly striking since this term typically describes human covenant loyalty toward God, creating what we might call "reciprocal faithfulness"—God demonstrates the same loyal love that He expects from humans. Traditional commentator Meiri noted that this verse answers potential objections to divine providence: if God cares for all creatures (verses 15-16), how can He be trusted to care particularly for the righteous? The answer lies in God's universal righteousness and faithfulness, which guarantee reliable care without compromising justice. The comprehensive scope (בְּכׇל, "in all") ensures that divine moral character has no exceptions or limitations, making God's accessibility (verse 18) grounded in perfect moral reliability.

**Verse 18**
The accessibility declaration קָר֣וֹב יְ֭הוָה לְכׇל־קֹרְאָ֑יו לְכֹ֤ל אֲשֶׁר־יִקְרָאֻ֨הוּ בֶאֱמֶֽת ("The LORD is near to all who call upon Him, to all who call upon Him in truth") creates a remarkable tension between divine transcendence (emphasized throughout the psalm) and divine accessibility. The adjective קָר֣וֹב (near) appears 77 times in Scripture, often describing physical proximity, but here it describes relational availability. The comprehensive scope לְכׇל־קֹרְאָ֑יו (to all who call upon Him) maintains the psalm's universalizing vision while introducing a participatory condition—divine nearness requires human calling.

The repetitive structure לְכֹ֤ל...לְכֹ֤ל (to all...to all) creates emphasis through doubling, while the qualification בֶאֱמֶֽת ("in truth") introduces a crucial condition for divine accessibility. The word אֱמֶת (truth/faithfulness) appears 126 times in Scripture, often suggesting reliability or sincerity rather than merely factual accuracy. The phrase בֶאֱמֶֽת functions as an adverbial qualifier specifying the manner of calling—with sincerity, genuineness, or integrity rather than mere lip service.

The verb קרא (to call) appears over 700 times in Scripture in various contexts: summoning, proclaiming, crying for help, or invoking. Here it suggests both invocation and cry for help, making divine accessibility available to any sincere petition. The repetition יִקְרָאֻ֨הוּ (they call upon Him) emphasizes the human initiative required for experiencing divine nearness—God's availability must meet human approach. This verse creates what we might call "universal accessibility with conditions"—divine nearness is available to all, but requires genuine seeking rather than casual acknowledgment. Traditional commentator Ibn Ezra noted that the condition בֶאֱמֶֽת prevents presumption: divine accessibility cannot be manipulated through insincere ritual but requires authentic relationship. The verse balances divine sovereignty (God's choice to be accessible) with human responsibility (calling upon God genuinely).

**Verse 19**
The promise רְצוֹן־יְרֵאָ֥יו יַעֲשֶׂ֑ה וְאֶת־שַׁוְעָתָ֥ם יִשְׁמַ֗ע וְיוֹשִׁיעֵֽם ("He will fulfill the desire of those who fear Him; He will also hear their cry and save them") presents a three-stage progression of divine responsiveness: fulfilling desire (יַעֲשֶׂ֑ה), hearing cry (יִשְׁמַ֗ע), and saving (וְיוֹשִׁיעֵֽם). The term רְצוֹן (desire/will) appears 56 times in Scripture, and its fulfillment by God suggests alignment between human desire and divine action when properly oriented toward God through יִרְאָה (fear/reverence).

The phrase יְרֵאָ֥יו (those who fear Him) specifies the recipients of this promise as those characterized by יִרְאַת יְהוָה (fear of the LORD), a concept appearing over 300 times in Scripture as the foundation of wisdom and proper relationship with God. The progression from רְצוֹן (desire) to שַׁוְעָה (cry for help) suggests movement from general longing to specific distress, with divine response encompassing both proactive fulfillment and reactive rescue.

The verb שמע (to hear) appears over 1160 times in Scripture, often emphasizing not merely auditory reception but responsive action. The culminating verb יוֹשִׁיעֵֽם (He will save them) from ישע (to save/deliver) provides the ultimate divine response—not merely hearing but effective intervention. This three-fold divine response (fulfill, hear, save) creates what we might call "comprehensive providence"—God addresses both the desires and the distresses of those who maintain proper reverent relationship. Medieval commentator Radak noted that this verse specifies the meaning of divine "nearness" (verse 18): practical responsiveness to human need when approached through proper reverence rather than presumption. The progression from desire-fulfillment to cry-hearing to salvation encompasses both positive provision and negative protection, making divine accessibility practically effective rather than merely theoretically available.

**Verse 20**
The protective promise שׁוֹמֵ֣ר יְ֭הוָה אֶת־כׇּל־אֹהֲבָ֑יו וְאֵ֖ת כׇּל־הָרְשָׁעִ֣ים יַשְׁמִֽיד ("The LORD preserves all who love Him, but all the wicked He will destroy") introduces the psalm's only negative element, creating moral dualism between אֹהֲבָ֑יו (those who love Him) and הָרְשָׁעִ֣ים (the wicked). The verb שׁמר (to guard/preserve) appears over 460 times in Scripture, often describing protective care, while the contrasting verb שמד (to destroy) appears 90 times, typically in contexts of divine judgment.

The contrast between אֹהֲבָ֑יו (those who love Him) and יְרֵאָ֥יו (those who fear Him) from verse 19 suggests different dimensions of proper relationship with God—reverent fear and loving devotion. The comprehensive scope כׇּל־אֹהֲבָ֑יו...כׇּל־הָרְשָׁעִ֣ים (all who love Him...all the wicked) maintains the psalm's universalizing vision while acknowledging moral distinctions that affect divine response.

This verse seems to contradict the psalm's emphasis on universal divine care (verse 9, "good to all"), creating interpretive tension. Traditional resolution comes through Rashi's insight that divine destruction of the wicked serves ultimately redemptive purposes—wickedness itself must be eliminated for universal flourishing to occur. The verse envisions not vindictive punishment but necessary removal of obstacles to comprehensive divine care. Medieval commentator Radak noted that this verse describes eschatological reality when evil will be eliminated, allowing full manifestation of the divine goodness celebrated throughout the psalm. The moral dualism functions not as limitation on divine care but as necessary condition for its ultimate effectiveness—the "wicked" who oppose divine goodness must be removed for universal blessing to flourish. This creates what we might call "protective universalism"—divine care for all requires elimination of those who systematically harm others, making judgment serve love rather than contradicting it.

**Verse 21**
The concluding declaration תְּהִלַּ֤ת יְהוָה֨ יְֽדַבֶּר־פִּ֗י וִיבָרֵ֣ךְ כׇּל־בָּ֭שָׂר שֵׁ֥ם קׇדְשׁ֗וֹ לְעוֹלָ֥ם וָעֶֽד ("My mouth shall speak the praise of the LORD, and all flesh shall bless His holy name forever and ever") creates perfect inclusio with verse 1 through the phrase לְעוֹלָ֥ם וָעֶֽד ("forever and ever"), while dramatically expanding the scope of praise from individual commitment to universal participation. The phrase כׇּל־בָּ֭שָׂר (all flesh) appears 32 times in Scripture, typically referring to all living beings including both humans and animals (Genesis 6:19; Isaiah 40:5; Joel 2:28).

The progression from יְֽדַבֶּר־פִּ֗י (my mouth shall speak) to וִיבָרֵ֣ךְ כׇּל־בָּ֭שָׂר (all flesh shall bless) creates what might be called "catalytic expansion"—individual commitment to praise becomes the catalyst for universal blessing. The verb דבר (to speak) suggests deliberate articulation, while ברך (to bless) implies acknowledging and proclaiming divine goodness. The phrase שֵׁ֥ם קׇדְשׁ֗וֹ (His holy name) emphasizes the divine reputation or character revealed through the acts celebrated throughout the psalm.

The title תְּהִלָּה (praise) from verse 1 returns in the phrase תְּהִלַּ֤ת יְהוָה (the praise of the LORD), creating perfect thematic inclusio while transforming the genre designation into content description—this "song of praise" becomes "the praise" that the psalmist commits to speak. The temporal phrase לְעוֹלָ֥ם וָעֶֽד completes the eternal framework established in verses 1-2, but now encompasses all flesh rather than merely individual commitment. This creates what we might call "democratic eschatology"—the psalm's vision culminates in universal praise where every creature participates in blessing the divine name. The progression from "I will extol" (verse 1) to "all flesh shall bless" (verse 21) traces the psalm's entire theological arc: personal commitment expanding through communal witness to cosmic chorus, making individual praise the seed of universal acknowledgment of divine kingship.

# Research Bundle for Psalm 145

## Hebrew Lexicon Entries (BDB)

### תְּהִלָּה
**Lexicon**: Klein Dictionary
**Vocalized**: תְּהִלָּה  

praise, glory. fame, renown. song of praise, psalm.

**Etymology**: [Verbal n. of הִלֵּל, Pi. of הלל ᴵ. For the form of this word see pref. תַּ◌. cp. Arab. tahlīl (= the shouting of a sacred formula).]  

---

### תְּהִלָּה
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: תְּהִלָּה  

n.f. praise, song of praise (cf. Arabic يَهْلِيلٌ, the shouting of a sacred formula; SprengerMuḥammad iii. 527 WeSkizzen iii. 107 ff. 114.117.214 NöZMG 1887, 723 RSSem i. 411 CheOP 460) abs. ת׳ Is 60:18 + 19 times + Je 49:25 Kt, v. infr.; cstr. תְּהִלַּת Je 48:2 + 2 times; sf. תְּהִלָּתִי Je 17:14 + 6 times; תְּהִלָּֽתְךָ Dt 10:21, ψ 48:11, תְּהִלָּתֶ֑ךָ ψ 35:28 + 6 times; תְּהִלָּתֶיךָ ψ 19:15; on תְּהִלָּ֫תֶ֥יךָ ψ 9:15 as error for -לָּ֫תֶ֥ךָ or -לֹּ֫תֶ֥יךָ, v. Ol§ 131 k; 39 c Sta§ 354 b Hup-Now al. ad. loc. תְּהִלָּתוֹ Is 42:10 + 10 times; pl. abs. תְּהִלֹּת Ex 15:11; cstr. תְּהִלּוֹת ψ 22:4 + 3 times;— praise, adoration, thanksgiving, paid to י׳:—ψ 48:11 כשׁמך אלהים כן תהלתך על קצוי ארץ as thy name, O God, so is thy praise to the ends of the earth, 111:10 תהלתו עמדת לעד, תָּמִיד תְּהִלָּתוֹ בְּפִי ψ 34:2 continually is his praise in my mouth (‖ אֲבָֽרֲכָה אֶת־י׳) cf. 71:6, also ימָּלֵא פִי תְהִלָּתֶ֑ךָ 71:8, פִּי יַגִּיד ת׳ 51:17, תַּבַּעְנָה שְׂפָתַי ת׳ 119:171, ת׳ י׳ יְדַבֶּר פִּי 145:21 (‖ וִיבָרֵךְ כָּל־בָּשָׂר שֵׁם קָדְשׁוֹ); as sung, song of praise יָשִׁירוּ תְּהִלָּתוֹ 106:12; הַמְשֹׁרֲרִים וְשִׁיר־תְּהִלָּה וְהוֹדוֹת לֵאלֹהִים Ne 12:46; cf. ת׳ לֵאלֹהֵינוּ ψ 40:4 (appos. שִׁיר חָדָשׁ) & Is 42:10 (appos. id.), also לַיְשָׁרִים נָאוָה ת׳ 33:1 (‖ רַנֲנוּ צַדִּיקִים בי׳); vid. perhaps ψ 148:14 (Che; yet see De VB); note further אֱלֹהֵי תְהִלָּתִי ψ 109:1; יוֹשֵׁב תְּהִלּוֹת יִשְׂרָאֵל ψ 22:4 inhabiting the praises of Isr.; וּמְרוֹמָם עַל־כָּל־בְּרָכָה וּתְהִלָּה Ne 9:5 exalted above all blessing and praise; cf. also תְּהִלָּתִי Is 48:9 (‖ שְׁמִי), & of praise due to י׳ Is 42:8. the act of general, public, praise (cf. also supr. especially ψ 22:4; 33:1; 106:12 Ne 12:46);—בֹּאוּ שְׁעָרָיו בְּתוֹדָה חֲצֵרֹתָיו בִּת׳ ψ 100:4 enter his gates with thanksgiving, his courts with praise; שִׁירוּ לי׳ שִׁיר חָדָשׁ תְּהִלָּתוֹ בִּקְהַל חֲסִידִים ψ 149:1; cf. ψ 22:26; 65:2; 66:2; 147:1; before the army הֵחֵלּוּ בְרִנָּה וּתְהִלָּה 2 Ch 20:22; in exhortation to the peoples הַשְׁמִיעוּ קוֹל תְּהִלָּתוֹ ψ 66:8 (‖ בָּֽרֲכוּ עַמִּים אֱלֹהֵינוּ), cf. Is 42:12. praise-song, as title, תְּהִלָּה לְדָוִד ψ 145:1 (so NH ספר תהלות & תְּהִלִּים, תִּילִּים, = Psalms). qualities, deeds, etc., of י׳, demanding praise:—נוֹרָא תְהִלֹּת Ex 15:11 (song), i.e. terrible in attributes that call for praise (‖ נֶאְדָּר בַּקֹּדֶשׁ & עֹשֵׁה פֶלֶא); often (both sg. and pl.) of Yahweh’s deeds c. vb. סִפֵּר ψ 9:15; 78:4; 79:13 Is 43:21, also + שֵׁם י׳ ψ 102:22; c. יַשְׁמִיעַ ψ 106:2 (‖ יְמַלֵּל גְּבוּרוֹת י׳); + צִדְקֶ֑ךָ c. יֶהְגֶּה ψ 35:28; c. יְבַשֵּׂ֑רוּ Is 60:6; + חַסְדֵי י׳, c. אַזְכִּיר Is 63:7; vid. also לְהֹדוֹת לְשֵׁם קָדְשֶׁ֑ךָ לְהִשְׁתַּבֵּחַ בִּתְהִלָּתֶ֑ךָ ψ 106:47 = 1 Ch 16:35 to give thanks unto thy holy name, to glory in thy praises (= praiseworthy deeds). renown, fame, glory: of Damascus עִיר תְּהִלָּ֑ה֯ Je 49:25 (on Qr תְּהִלָּ֑ת v. Hi Ges§ 80. 2 b); comp. perhaps מַעֲטֵה תְהִלָּה Is 61:3 mantle of renown (renown as a mantle, cf. De VB; > Di splendid garment; but Bi Che Du read מַעֲטֵה אֵ֔בֶל and translate ת׳ praise, song of praise, cf. 1. 2). of י׳, כִּסָּה שָׁמַים הוֹדוֹ וּת׳ מָֽלְאָה הָאָרֶץ Hb 3:3 his splendour hath covered (the) heavens, and of his renown the earth is full. Also fig., praise, renown, = obj. of praise, possessor of renown; used (a) of Israel Dt 26:19 (abs.), Je 13:11 (+ לִי) 33:9 (+ לִי and לְכֹל גּוֹיֵי הָאָרֶץ)—all ‖ שֵׁם, תִּפְאֶרֶת; v. also Is 61:11, & perhaps ψ 148:14 (see sub 1 supr.); (b) of Jerusalem Is 62:7, also Zp 3:19, 20 (both ‖ שֵׁם), all these c. vb. הָיָה or שִׂים; cf. וְקָרָאת יְשׁוּעָה חוֹמֹתַיִךְ וּשְׁעָרַיִךְ ת׳ Is 60:18; (c) of Babylon וַתִּתָּפֵשׂ תְּהִלַּת כָּל־הָאָרֶץ Je 51:41; (d) of Moab אֵין עוֹד ת׳ מוֹאָב Je 48:2; (e) of י׳, הוּא תְהִלָּֽתְךָ וְהוּא אֱלֹהֶיךָ Dt 10:21, כִּי תְהִלָּתִי אָ֑תָּה Je 17:14 (no vb.)

### אֲרוֹמִמְךָ
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: רוּם²  

n.[m.] height, haughtiness (proposes inf.);—abs. ר׳, height, loftiness, שָׁמַיִם לָר׳ וָאָרֶץ לָעֹמֶק Pr 25:3; cstr. רוּם עֵינַיִם fig. of haughtiness Is 10:12 (‖ גֹּדֶל לֵבָב), Pr 21:4 (‖ רְחַב־לֵב); so רֻם לִבּוֹ Je 48:29 (‖ גַּאֲוָה, גָּאוֹן, גֹּבַתּ); then alone: haughtiness, רוּם אֲנָשִׁים Is 2:11, 17 (both ‖ גַּבְהוּת).

### אֲרוֹמִמְךָ
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: רָם  

n.pr.m. an ancestor of David, Ru 4:19(×2), brother of Jerachmeel 1 Ch 2:9, 10. son of J. 1 Ch 2:25, 27.—Αρραν, Ραμ, etc. name of Elihu’s family, Jb 32:2 (Ραμ[α], Αραμ), dub., cf. Bu.

### חֵקֶר
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: חֵ֫קֶר  

n.m. Ju 5:16 searching, thing (to be) searched out;—abs. הֵ֫קֶר Jb 5:9 + 8 times; cstr. id. Pr 25:27 + 2 times; pl. cstr. חִקְרֵי Ju 5:16;—חִקְרֵי לֵב Ju 5:16 searchings, questionings of heart; Jb 11:7 canst thou reach ח׳ אֱלוֹהַּ i.e. what is to be explored in him, the whole range of his nature, so ח׳ תהום 38:16 the range of the deep; especially אֵין ח׳ i.e. it is unsearchable Jb 5:9; 9:10 ψ 145:3 Pr 25:3 Is 40:28, cf. לא ח׳ Jb 34:24; 36:26; ח׳ אבותם 8:8 i.e. thing searched out by their fathers.

### הֲדַר
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: הָדָר  

n.m. ψ 149:9 ornament, splendour, honour (chiefly poet.)—abs. ה׳ Dt 33:17 + 12 times; cstr. הֲדַר Is 2:10 + 7 times; sf. הֲדָרִי Mi 2:9 (but cf. infr.) Ez 16:14; הֲדָרְךָ ψ 45:5; 90:16; הֲדָרֶ֑ךָ ψ 45:4; הֲדָרֵךְ Ez 27:10; הֲדָרָהּ Is 5:14 La 1:6; pl. cstr. הַדְרֵי ψ 110:3 (but read prob. הררי so Symm Jer edd.; Ol Hup Gr Bi Che);— ornament Pr 20:29 (grey hair, for old men; ‖ תִּפְאֶרֶת), Ez 16:14 (fig. of ornaments of Jerus. as bride of י׳); פְּרִי עֵץ הָדָר Lv 23:40 (H) i.e. fruit of goodly (ornamental, beautiful) trees, so AV RV & most, or goodly tree-fruit, 𝔊 καρπὸν ξύλου ὡραῖον, so Di;—here would come also בהדרי קדשׁ ψ 110:3, ref. to sacred, festal garments, according to Thes al. cf. RVm; but read prob. on the holy mountains, vid. supr. splendour, majesty Dt 33:17 of Ephraim under figure of a noble bull (cf. Di); of Jerusalem Is 5:14 cf. La 1:6; of Carmel Is 35:2 (‖ כָּבוֹד); of majesty & dignity conferred by י׳ on man ψ 8:6 (‖ כָּבוֹד), on king 21:6 (‖ id., הוֹד), cf. 45:4 (‖ הוֹד) & v 5 (but text perhaps erron. Checrit. n.); denied of suffering servant of י׳ Is 53:2 (‖ תֹּאַר); of dignity of good & capable woman (אֵשֶׁת חַיִל) Pr 31:25 (‖ עֹז); of splendour due to warlike equipment Ez 27:10; especially of majesty of י׳ 1 Ch 16:27 = ψ 96:6, cf. Jb 40:10 ψ 111:3 (all ‖ הוֹד); also ψ 104:1 (‖ id., under fig. of garment); further ψ 29:4 (‖ כֹּחַ), 90:16 (‖ פֹּעַל); in combination הֲדַר כְּבוֹד הוֹדְךָ ψ 145:5; כְּבוֹד ה׳ v 12; הֲדַר גְּאֹנוֹ Is 2:10, 19, 21. honour, glory ψ 149:9 for saints of י׳; cf. Mi 2:9 of י׳’s glory as possession of his people, lost by exile & slavery: but perhaps textual error; HoffmZAW 182, 103 proposes הַדְּרוֹר, from their children ye take freedom (yet מֵעַל then hardly suitable).

### הוֹד
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: הוֹד  

n.m. Jb 37:22 splendour, majesty, vigour (chiefly poet.) (NH id.)—ה׳ abs. Jb 37:22 + 8 times (הַה׳ 1 Ch 29:11); cstr. Is 30:30 + 3 times; sf. הוֹדִי Dn 10:8; הוֹדְךָ Nu 27:20 + 2 times; הוֹדֶ֑ךָ Pr 5:9 ψ 145:4; הוֹדוֹ Ho 14:7 + 3 times; הֹדֹה Je 22:18;— splendour, majesty, of king הוֹדְךָ וַהֲדָרֶ֑ךָ ψ 45:4; הוֹד וְהָדָר תְּשַׁוֶּה עָלָיו ψ 21:6, subj. י׳ (‖ גָּדוֹל כְּבוֹדוֹ בִּישׁוּעָתֶ֑ךָ); foretold of the צֶמַה Zc 6:13 וְהוּא־יִשָּׂא הוֹד וְיָשַׁב וּמָשַׁל עַל־כִּסְאוֹ; cf. וַיִּתֵּן עָלָיו הוֹד מַלְכוּת 1 Ch 29:25 (י׳ confers on Sol.); וְלֹא נָֽתְנוּ עָלָיו ה׳ מַלְכוּת Dn 11:21 (of usurper); v. also וְהוֹי הֹדֹה Je 22:18 in lament for king. Hence also divine splendour, majesty, light & glory which God wears as king: הוֹד וְהָדָר לָבָ֑שְׁתָּ ψ 104:1 (7 times joined with הָדָר); cf. Jb 40:10 where י׳ addresses Job in irony; עַל־אֱלוֹהַּ נוֹרָא הוֹד Jb 37:22; ה׳ וְהָדָר לְפָנָיו ψ 96:6 = 1 Ch 16:26; also הַגְּדֻלָּה וְהַגְּבוּרָה וְהַתִּפְאֶרֶת וְהַנֵּצַח וְהַה׳ 1 Ch 29:11; see further כִּסָּה שָׁמַיִם הוֹדוֹ Hb 3:3 (‖ וּתְהִלָּתוֹ מָֽלְאָה הָאָ֑רֶץ); אֲשֶׁר־תְּנָה הוֹדְךָ עַל־הַשָּׁמַיִם ψ 8:2 (‖ מָה־אַדִּיר שִׁמְךָ בְּכָל־הָאָ֑רֶץ); also הוֹדוֹ עַל־אֶרֶץ וְשָׁמָ֑יִם ψ 148:13 (‖ נִשְׂגָּב שְׁמוֹ לְבַדּוֹ); הֲדַר כְּבוֹד הוֹדֶ֑ךָ 145:4; of Yahweh’s actions הוֹד וְהָדָר פָּעֳלוֹ 111:3; of his voice וְהִשְׁמִיעַ י׳ אֶת־הוֹד קוֹלוֹ Is 30:30. splendour of Israel under divine blessing וִיהִי כַזַּיִת הוֹדוֹ Ho 14:7. majesty, dignity, authority of Moses Nu 27:20 (P; only here in Hex) וְנָתַתָּ מֵהוֹדְךָ עָלָיו. Also, manly vigour פֶּן־תִּתֵּן לַאֲחֵרִים הוֹדֶ֑ךָ Pr 5:9, in warning against licentiousness; as displayed in outward appearance וְהוֹדִי נֶהְפַּךְ עָלַי לְמַשְׁחִית Dn 10:8 (‖ כֹּחַ). of a horse, majesty, majestic force הוֹד נַחְרוֹ אֵימָה Jb 39:20 the majesty of his snorting (majestic snorting, VB) is a terror; also in sim. of Yahweh’s dealings with Judah, וְשָׂם אוֹתָם כְּסוּס הוֹדוֹ בַּמִּלְחָמָה Zc 10:3 and he will make them like the horse of his majesty in battle.

### יַבִּיעוּ
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: נָבַע  

vb. flow, spring, bubble up, poet. and late (NH id. (rare); Assyrian nabû, id., DlHWB 442; Arabic نَبَعَ well or issue forth (of water); Aramaic ܢܒܰܥ, נְבַע; Ethiopic ነብዐ ii. weep, አንብዕ tear(s));— Pt. נַ֫חַל נֹבֵעַ Pr 18:4 a flowing wady, metaph. of מְקוֹר חָכְמָה. Impf. יַבִּיעַ ψ 19:3 + 3 times; אַבִּ֫יעָה ψ 78:2 Pr 1:23; יַבִּ֫יעוּ ψ 94:4; 145:7; יַבִּיעוּן ψ 59:8; 3 fpl. תַּבַּ֫עְנָה ψ 119:171;— pour out, emit, cause to bubble, belch forth: pour out, fig. אַבִּיעָה לָכֶם רוּחִי Pr 1:23 (‖ אוֹדִ֫יעָה דְבָרַי אֶתְכֶם). cause to bubble, ferment זְבוּבֵי מָ֫וֶת יַבְאִישׁ יַבִּיעַ שֶׁמֶן רוֹקֵ֑חַ Ec 10:1. fig., usually of speech, pour forth, emit, belch forth, emphat. expression: פִּי רְשָׁעִים יַבִּיעַ רָעוֹת Pr 15:28 (opp. לֵב צַדִּיק יֶהְגֶּה לַעֲנוֹת) i.e. the righteous considers before he speaks, the wicked bursts out with reckless utterance; פִּי כְסִילִים יַבִּיעַ אִוֶּלֶת Pr 15:2 (opp. לְשׁוֹן חֲכָמִים תֵּיטִיב דָּ֑עַת); יַבִּ֫יעוּ יְדַבְּרוּ עָתָק ψ 94:4 (‖ יִתְאַמְּרוּ כָּל־פֹּעֲלֵי אָ֑וֶן); יַבּ׳ בְּפִיהֶם 59:8 (‖ חֲרָבוֹת בְּשִׂפְתוֹתֵיהֶם); in good sense, אַבִּ֫יעָה חִידוֹת 78:2 (‖ אֶפְתְּחָה בְּמָשָׁל פִּי); תַּבַּעְנָה שְׂפָתַי תְּהִלָּה 119:171; זֵכֶר רַב־טוּבְךָ יַבִּ֑יעוּ 145:7 (‖ וְצִדְקָֽתְךָ יְרַנֵּ֑נוּ, cf. also v 6); metaph. יוֹם לְיוֹם יַבּ׳ אֹמֶר 19:3, i.e. though silent, it really pours forth speech.

### אֶרֶךְ אַפַּיִם
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: אַף³  

conj. denoting addition, especially of something greater, also, yea (so Ph. Aramaic ܐܳܦ, אַף, אוֹף; cf. فَ ). very rare in plain prose (in which גַּם is more usual): Gn 40:16 (with pron., as rather often) I also in my dream, Nu 16:14 Dt 2:11, 20 2 S 20:14 (v. Dr) 2 K 2:14 Est 5:12; more freq. in poetry, especially as introducing emphatically a new thought Dt 33:3, 20, 28 1 S 2:7 ψ 16:6, 7, 9; 18:49; 65:14 they shout for joy, yea, they sing! 68:9, 17; 74:16; 89:28; 93:1 Pr 22:19; 23:28 +; or in more elevated prose style, Lv 26:16, 24, 28, 41; and 25 times in the impassioned rhetoric of Is 2 (40:24–48:15), e.q. 40:24; 41:10, 26; 42:13; 43:7, 19; 46:11 yea, I have spoken, I will also bring it to pass; I have purposed, I will also do it! 48:12, 15. Implying something surprising or unexpected, even, indeed Jb 14:3; 15:4. וְאַף and also Lv 26:39, 40, 42, 44 Dt 15:17 Hb 2:15 ψ 68:19 1 Ch 8:32 = 9:38 2 Ch 12:5 Ne 2:18; 13:15; and even Jb 19:4 וְאַף־אָמְנָם שָׁגִיתִי and even indeed (if) I have erred … With הֲ, הַאַף indeed …? really …? †Gn 18:13, 23 wilt thou indeed sweep away the righteous with the wicked? v 24 Am 2:11 Jb 34:17; 40:8. In contrast to a preceding thought (expressed or implied) but, nay ψ 44:10; 58:3; cf. Ju 5:29. (Equally in prose and poetry) with ref. to a preceding sentence, yea, à fortiori, the more so (= how much more! after an affirm. clause; = how much less! after a neg. one): †2 S 4:10 f. when one told me, Saul is dead … I took hold of him and slew him … אַף כִּי־אֲנָשִׁים רְשָׁעִים הָרְגוּ וג׳ à fortiori, how much more (should I do so), when wicked men have slain a righteous person, etc.! Ez 14:21 (Ew Hi) 15:5 Pr 21:27 (in all these passages כִּי = when) Jb 4:19. So וְאַף †1 S 23:3 2 K 5:13 … וְאַף כִּי־אָמַר אֵלֶיךָ and the more (= and how much rather), when he hath said to thee, etc. More commonly in this sense strengthened by כִּי (q.v.), v. infr.

---

### אֶרֶךְ אַפַּיִם
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: אָרַךְ  

vb. be long, almost always of time (Assyrian arâku COTGloss, Aramaic אֲרַךְ, ܐܶܪܰܟ )— Pf. אָֽרְכוּ Gn 26:8; Impf. יַאַרְכוּ Ez 12:22; 3 fpl. וַתֶּאֱרַכְנָה Ez 31:5 (del. B Co)—be long, subj. הַיָּמִים (i.e. a long time passed Gn 26:8 (J); cf. Assyrian urriku ûmî, days grew long, Creation Tableta v. COT Gn 1:1); of delayed fulfilment of prophecy Ez 12:22; subj. פֹּארֹת Ez 31:5 (but cf. supr.) Pf. הֶאֱרִיךְ Pr 19:11; וְהַאֲרַכְתָּ֫ Dt 22:7, etc.; Impf. יַאֲרִיךְ Dt 17:20 +; יַאֲרִיכוּ Dt 25:15; יַאֲרִכוּן Ex 20:12 cf. Dt 5:16; 6:2; תַּאֲרִיכֻן Dt 4:26; 30:18, etc.; Imv. fs. הַאֲרִיכִי Is 54:2: Inf. cstr. הַאֲרִיךְ Nu 9:19, 22; Pt. מַאֲרִיךְ Ec 7:15; 8:12;— trans. prolong, (a) obj. יָמִים (i.e. live long) Dt 4:26, 40; 5:30; 11:9; 17:20; 22:7; 30:18; 32:47 Jos 24:31 = Ju 2:7 (c. אַחֲרֵי = survive), Pr 28:16; Is 53:10; Ec 8:13; also (late) without ימים Ec 7:15; 8:12; (b) id. 1 K 3:14 י׳ subj. וְהַאֲרַכְתִּי אֶת־יָמֶיךָ I will prolong thy days; (c) postpone anger Is 48:9 cf. Pr 19:11 (i.e. shew oneself slow to anger); v. also Jb 6:11 אַאֲרִיךְ נַפְשִׁי i.e. be patient; (d) lit. (but in fig.) make long furrows (c. ל) ψ 129:3; tentcords Is 54:2; tongue 57:4 (stretch out in mockery). intrans. grow long, continue long (i.e. display length or continuance), subj. יָמִים Ex 20:12 = Dt 5:16; 6:2; 25:15; tarry long Nu 9:19, 22 (subj. הֶעָנָן), last (continue) long, subj. כֵּן Pr 28:2; be long (lit.) of staves of ark 1 K 8:8; 2 Ch 5:9.

### מַעֲשָׂיו
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: מַעֲשֶׂה²  

n.m. Gn 20:9 deed, work;—abs. מ׳ Gn 44:15 +, cstr. מַעֲשֵׂה Is 5:12 +; sf. מַעֲשֵׂהוּ Ju 13:12 +, etc.; pl. מַעֲשִׂים Gn 20:9 +; cstr. מַעֲשֵׂי Je 1:16 +; sf. מַעֲשָׂיו 1 S 19:4 (or sg., so Dr) +, etc.;—often acc. cogn. c. עשׂה;— (1) deed, thing done by man Nu 16:28 (JE), 1 K 13:11; Is 26:12;—1 S 20:19 is obscure; (2) implic. evil Gn 44:15 (J), Ex 23:24 (E), Ne 6:14 + 8 times, + sg. coll. doing(s) Mi 6:16; Lv 18:3(×2) (H), 2 Ch 17:4, מ׳ יְדֵיהֶם La 3:64; Hg 2:14; Jb 33:17 (reading מִמַּעֲשֵׂהוּ 𝔊 Di Siegf Bu), Ez 16:30 (behaviour); explic. מַעֲשֵׂינוּ הָרָעִים Ezr 9:13 cf. Ec 4:3; 8:11, 14, מַעֲשֵׂי־אָוֶן Is 59:6; specif. of idolatr. practices, מ׳ יְדֵיכֶם sg. Dt 31:29; Je 25:6, 7; 32:30, pl. 44:8, cf. sg. 1 K 16:7; 2 K 22:17 = 2 Ch 34:25; (3) implic. right, praiseworthy 1 S 19:4; 2 K 23:19; Jon 3:10; Pr 31:31; of duty in gen. הַמּ׳ אֲשֶׁר יַעֲשׂוּן Ex 18:20 (E) = what they are to do; explic. Ec 4:4; 8:14; (4) as basis of judgment (good or bad) Is 66:18 ψ 33:15; 62:13; Ec 12:14, מ׳ יְדֵיהֶם Je 25:14. (1) work, labour, husbandry Ex 23:16 (E), Gn 5:29 (J), Ju 19:16, מ׳ יְדֵיכֶם Hg 2:17; enforced labour of Isr. in Egypt Ex 5:5, 13 (J); in gen. 23:12 (E); also of product of labour 23:16, cf. Is 65:22; Ec 5:5; שֵׁשֶׁת יְמֵי הַמּ׳ Ez 46:1 the six working-days; of religious work, מ׳ עֲבֹדַת בֵּית הָא׳ 1 Ch 23:28, cf. מ׳ 2 Ch 31:21; (2) business, pursuit, Gn 46:33; 47:3 (J), Ju 13:12; 1 S 25:2; Is 54:16; in gen., occupation Is 29:15; Ec 2:4, 11; 3:17, 22; 8:9; 9:7, 10; (3) undertaking, enterprise Is 19:14, 15; Dt 15:10; מ׳ יָדֶ֑ךָ Dt 2:7 + 5 times Dt; 2 Ch 32:30; Jb 1:10 ψ 28:4, מ׳ יָדֵינוּ ψ 90:17(×2); (4) achievement Je 48:7; Est 10:2; (5) in weakened sense, מַעֲשֶׂיךָ Pr 16:3 thine affairs; nearly = occurrences Ec 1:14 (pl.), 2:17; 8:17 (sg.). deed(s), work(s) of י׳, especially in deliverance and in judgment, Ju 2:7, 10; Jos 24:31 (D), ψ 33:4 + 21 times, + מ׳ יָדָיו etc. Is 5:12 ψ 28:5; 92:5 + 4 times; מ׳ הָאֱלֹהִים in gen. Ec 7:13; 8:17; 11:5 cf. 3:11. (1) work, thing made, by man, מ׳ אֹפֶה Gn 40:17 (E) baker’s work; מ׳ יְדֵי יוֹצֵר La 4:2; often of furnishings of tabern., מ׳ חשֵׁב Ex 26:1 + 7 times, מ׳ רֹקֵם 26:36 + 5 times, מ׳ אֹרֵג 28:32 + 2 times, מ׳ חָרָשׁ 28:11, cf. מ׳ יְדֵי אָמָּן Ct 7:2; of holy oil, etc. מ׳ רֹקֵחַ Ex 30:25 + 2 times (ins. רֹקֵחַ perhaps also 2 Ch 16:14); hence מ׳ = work of art Is 3:24 (of coiffure), cf. כְּלִי מ׳ Nu 31:51; sq. n. of material, pattern, etc., מ׳ עִזִּים v 20 (i.e. goats’ hair), v. also Ex 27:4; 28:14 +; in temple, מ׳ שְׂבָכָה 1 K 7:17, מ׳ שׁוּשַׁן v 19, etc.; מ׳ צַעֲצֻעִים 2 Ch 3:10, cf. מ׳ הַחֲבִתִּים 1 Ch 9:31 the work of (consisting in) bread-wafers; = materials of (prepared for) הָעוֹלָה 2 Ch 4:6; in theoph. מ׳ לִבְנַת הַסַּפִּיר Ex 24:10 (J); מ׳ of ships 2 Ch 20:37; in gen. Is 29:16; Ez 27:16, 18 = thy manufactures, wares; מַעֲשַׂי ψ 45:2 = my verses (ποίημα, poem); (2) workmanship Ez 1:16(×2) (v 16a del. ### חֲסִידֶיךָ
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: חָסִיד  

adj.m. kind, pious (so, as denoting active practice of חֶסֶד, kindness, Thes MV De and most, cf. קָצִיר, פָּקִיד etc.; > Hup on ψ 4:4 RVm who expl. as passive reception of י׳’s חֶסֶד, cf. שָׂכִיר, אָסִיר etc.; its use as attribute of God Je 3:12 ψ 145:17, and the context ψ 12:1 Mi 7:2 etc., favour active sense)—ח׳ ψ 4:4 + 9 times; חֲסִידֶ֑ךָ Dt 33:8 ψ 89:20; חֲסִידְיךָ֯ ψ 16:10; pl. חֲסִידִים ψ 149:1, 5; sf. חֲסִידָ֑י ψ 50:5; חֲסִידָו֯ 1 S 2:9 + 15 times sfs.;— kind: of man עם ח׳ תתחסד with the kind thou shewest thyself kind 2 S 22:26 = ψ 18:26. of wing of ostrich אִם אֶבְרָה חֲסִידָה Jb 39:13 is it a kindly pinion? poss. with play on חֲסִידָה n.f. stork (is the ostrich kind like the stork?). of God, only Je 3:12 ψ 145:17. pious, godly, either as exhibition of ‘duteous love’ toward God (CheOP 378), or (in view of rarity of such passages as Ho 6:4, 6; Je 2:2, and their possible ambiguity) because kindness, as prominent in the godly, comes to imply other attributes, and to be a designation of the godly character, piety: as adj.—גּוֹי לא חסיד a nation, not pious, = ungodly ψ 43:1. elsewhere as subst.: sing., a pious man, the godly ψ 4:4; 12:2; 32:6; 86:2, ‖ יָשָׁר Mi 7:2; (thy) pious one(s) ψ 16:10 (Kt pl.), 1 S 2:9 (Qr pl.) Pr 2:8, אִישׁ חֲסִידֶ֑ךָ men of thy pious one Dt 33:8 (Moses, v. Di; others, the man, thy godly one, i.e. Levi); pl. the pious, godly, those of the people who were faithful, devoted to God’s service, only in Psalter and chiefly, if not entirely, in late Psalms ψ 149:1, 5; his pious ones ψ 30:5; 31:24; 37:28; 85:9; 97:10; 116:15; 148:14; 149:9; thy pious ones ψ 52:11; 79:2; 89:20; 132:9 = 2 Ch 6:41, ψ 145:10; my pious ones ψ 50:5, her (Zion’s) pious ψ 132:16.—(In Maccab. age, συναγωγὴ Ἀσιδαίων denoted, technically, the party of the pious, who opposed the Hellenization of Judaea, v. 1 Macc 2:42; cf. 7:13 2 Macc 14:6 and CheOP 48, 56; so perhaps ψ 116:15; 149:1, 5, 9.)

---

### לִבְנֵי הָאָדָם
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: בֵּן  

n.m. son (MI Ph. בן; so Sab. CISiv. No. 2, cf. בני DHMSem. Sprachforsch. 6; Arabic اِبْنٌ; Assyrian bin(u), LyonSargon 9, 1. 57; especially in bin-bin, grandson COTGloss., cf. Dl infr.; Aramaic בַּר, ܒܰܪ, pl. בְּנִין, ܒܢܺܝܢ; cf. Palm., especially VogNo. 21. 31. 36 a al.; possibly orig. connected with בנה build, so Thes, cf. Assyrian bânu, begetter (DlPr 104 & cf. BaZMG 1887, 638 ff.); but all traces of this √ lost in Heb. form; √ perhaps orig. bilit. (בִּן, בֵּן) בַּן v. Sta§ 183)—abs. בּ׳ Gn 4:25 +; בֵּֽן־ Ez 18:10; cstr. בֵּן Gn 49:22(×2); בֶּן־ Gn 5:32 +; בֶּן Est 2:5; Ne 6:18, & c. prefix Gn 17:17; Nu 8:25; 1 Ch 27:23; 2 Ch 25:5; 31:16, 17; בְּנוֹ Nu 23:18; 24:3, 15; בְּנִי Gn 49:11; בִּן Dt 25:2; בִּן־ Ex 33:11 + 32 times (29 times in combination בִּן־נוּן (ישׁוע, הושׁע) יהושׁע); sf. בְּנִי Gn 21:10 +; בִּנְךָ Ex 20:10 +; לִבֱנ֑ךָ Dt 7:3; 1 K 11:13; בְּנֵךְ Gn 30:14 +; בְּנוֹ Gn 4:17 +; בְּנָהּ Gn 21:10 +; pl. בָּנִים Gn 3:16 +; cstr. בְּנֵי Gn 6:2 +; sf. בָּנַי Gn 31:43 +; בָּנֵינוּ Jos 22:25 +; בְּנֵיכֶם Ex 3:22 +, etc.;— son, male child, born of a woman Gn 4:25; 16:11, 15; 17:19 cf. v 16 18:10, 14; 19:37, 38 + often, cf. בֶּן־בִּטְנָהּ Is 49:15; begotten by a man Gn 5:4 f. 28; 6:10; 11:11 f. + often; ‖ בַּת (בָּנוֹת) daughter Gn 5:4, 7, 10 f. Ex 20:10 Dt 5:14; 16:11, 14 1 S 30:3, 6 Jb 1:2 42:13 +; of son as desired Gn 30:2 (cf. 15:2; 16:2; 17:17; 18:10 f. 1 S 1:5–11) 2 K 4:14, 28 ψ 127:3 +; rejoiced in Gn 30:6 +; beloved Ex 21:5 2 S 19:1, 3, 5 1 K 3:26; cared for Dt 1:31; spared Mal 3:17; disciplined & trained Dt 8:5 Pr 3:12; 13:24; 19:18; 29:17; owing reverence, obedience, etc. to parents Pr 6:20; 10:1; 13:1; בִּנְךָ בְּכוֹרְךָ thy first-born son Gn 27:32; הַבֵּן הַבְּכֹר Dt 21:15 cf. 1 S 8:2; בְּנָהּ הַגָּדֹל her elder son Gn 27:15, 42; בְּנוֹ הַגָּדֹל 27:1; בְּנָהּ הַקָּטָן her younger son Gn 27:15, 42. In partic. בֶּן־אִמּוֹ son of his mother, i.e. own (uterine) brother Gn 43:29, cf. 27:29 Ju 8:19 ψ 50:20; 69:9, & v. אֵם; בְּנֵי אָבִיךָ son of thy father = brethren Gn 49:8 (poet.) בְּנֵי דֹדֵיהֶן = cousins Nu 36:11. בְּנִי my son, as term of kindliness of endearment, used by Eli to Samuel 1 S 3:6, 16 cf. 4:16; 24:17; 26:17, 21, 25, v. also Pr 1:8, 10 2:1 +; cf. בִּנְךָ, used by Benhaded of himself to Elisha 2 K 8:9; by Ahaz to Tiglath-pileser 16:7; especially to express intimate and gracious relation with God: י׳ calls Israel בְּנִי בְכֹרִי Ex 4:22 cf. v 23 Ho 11:1, v. also ψ 80:16 (but cf. Che); בָּנִים אַתֶּם ליהוה אלהיכם Dt 14:1; בְּנֵי עֶלְיוֹן ψ 82:6 (‖ אלהים); בְּנֵי אֵל־חָ֑י Ho 2:1; cf. further Dt 32:5 (pl.) v 20 (pl.) Is 1:2, 4; 30:1, 9; Je 3:14, 22; 4:22; 31:20; of future Davidic king 2 S 7:14 = 1 Ch 17:13 cf. ψ 2:7; expressly referred to Solomon 1 Ch 22:10; 28:6; also of children (offered in fire) Ez 16:21. בְּנֵי האלהים applied to supernatural beings Gn 6:2, 4; Jb 1:6; 2:1; בְּנֵי אלהים Jb 38:7; בְּנֵי אֵלִים ψ 29:1 (on which cf. Che’s note) 89:7. בֶּן־אָדָם son of man, cf. בְּנֵי א׳, v. אָדָם; †בְּנֵי אִישׁ ψ 4:3 & (‖ בני אדם) 49:3; 62:10; La 3:33. בֶּן־בִּנְךָ = thy grandson Ex 10:2; Dt 6:2; Ju 8:22 cf. Je 27:7; also pl. Ex 34:7; Dt 4:9, 25; Ju 12:14; 2 K 17:41; 1 Ch 8:40; Jb 42:16; ψ 128:6; Pr 13:22; 17:6; Ez 37:25; also בֵּן alone with similar reference Gn 29:5 (Laban son of Nahor); Laban calls his daughters’ children his own sons Gn 31:28, 43 cf. 32:1; son of Naomi Ru 4:17; בְּנֵי רְבִעִים 2 K 10:30 sons of the fourth generation, and, in general, descendants Jos 22:24, 25, 27 +; cf. 2 S 19:25 2 K 9:20; Ez 5:1. constantly, as more precise designation, added to personal name כָּלֵב בֶּן־יְפֻנֶּה Nu 14:30; 32:12; 34:19 +; יְהוֹשֻׁעַ בִּן־נוּן Nu 11:28; 14:30; 32:12, 28; 34:17 +; יָרָבְעָם בֶּן־נְבָט 1 K 12:2, 15 +, etc.; also without personal name (often with implication of contempt) בֶּן־קִישׁ 1 S 10:11; בֶּן־יִשַׁי 1 S 20:27, 30, 31; 22:7, 8, 9, 13; 25:10 2 S 20:1; בְּנֵי צְרוּיָה 2 S 16:10; בֶּן־רְמַלְיָהוּ Is 7:4, 5, 9; 8:16; בֶּן־טָֽבְאַ֑ל Is 7:6; cf. also בְּנֵי לֵוִי Nu 16:7, 8. designated as בֶּן־זְקֻנִים i.e. born in old age of father Gn 37:3; opp. בְּנֵי הַנְּעוּרִים sons of one’s youth ψ 127:4; also בֶּן־בֵּיתִי one born in my house Gn 15:3 (i.e. slave) so בְּנֵי בַיִת Ec 2:7. in various combinations: (α) as expression of contumely, בֶּן־נַעֲוַת הַמַּרְדּוּת 1 S 20:30; בֶּן־הַמְּרַצֵּחַ הַוֶּה 2 K 6:32 this son of a murderer; cf. בְּנֵי־נָבָל Jb 30:8; בְּנֵי בְלִי־שֵׁם ib.; בְּנֵי עֹנֲנָה Is 57:3 (‖ זֶרַע מְנָאֵף); cf. בֶּן־אִשָּׁה אַחֶרֶת Ju 11:2 (cf. v 1); (β) as term of respect, dignity, בֶּן־חוֹרִים son of nobles Ec 10:17 (in Aramaic = free born); בֶּן־חֲכָמִים Is 19:11; בֶּן־מַלְכֵי־קֶדֶם ib.; cf. בֶּן־מֶלֶךְ ψ 72:1 (‖ מֶלֶךְ); בֶּן־אֲמָתֶ֑ךָ ψ 86:16 in addressing י׳ (‖ עַבְדֶּ֑ךָ) & בְּנֵי־עֲבָדֶיךָ ψ 102:29; of noble appearance בְּנֵי הַמֶּלֶךְ Ju 8:18. often pl. with name of ancestor, people, land, or city, to denote descendants, inhabitants, membership in a nation or family, etc.: (α) e.g. בְּנֵי־עֵבֶר Gn 10:21; בְּנֵי־חֵת Gn 23:3, 5, 7, 10(×2), 11, 16, 18, 20; 25:10; 49:32 (all P); (בְּנֵי־שֵׁת) Nu 24:17 v. sub 8); בְּנֵי־חֲמוֹר Gn 33:19 Jos 24:32; בְּנֵי עֵשָׂו Gn 36:5, 15, 19 Dt 2:4, 8, 12, 22, 29; בְּנֵי שֵׂעִיר Gn 36:20, 21; בֶּן־(בני)הִנֹּם Jos 15:8 + (cf. sub גַּיְא); בְּנֵי לוֹט Dt 2:9, 19 ψ 83:9; בְּנֵי־יוֹסֵף (lit. Gn 46:27; 48:8; 1 Ch 5:1) Nu 1:32; 26:28, 37; 34:23; 36:5 (מַטֵּה ב׳ י׳) + 6 times Jos, cf. ψ 77:16; even בְּנֵי חֲצִי שֵׁבֶט מְנַשֶּׁה 1 Ch 5:23; בְּנֵי דָוִיד (lit. 2 S 8:18 = 1 Ch 18:17, 1 Ch 3:1, 9) 2 Ch 13:8; 23:3; 32:33; בְּנֵי אָסָף 2 Ch 29:13; Ezr 2:41; 3:8 + (v. אָסָף); בְּנֵי קֹרַח in titles of ψ 42–49, 84, 85, 87, 88; especially (β) בְּנֵי־עַמּוֹן (standing designation of people of Ammon) Gn 19:38 + 81 times (cf. עַמּוֹן & NöZMG 1886, 171 DrSm 66); בְּנֵי יַעֲקֹב (lit. Gn 34:7, 13, 25, 27; 35:5, 22, 26; 49:2) 2 K 17:34 ψ 105:6 Mal 3:6 cf. ψ 77:16; & chiefly (γ) בְּנֵי יִשְׂרָאֵל (lit. Gn 42:5; 45:21; 46:5; Ex 1:1) Ex 1:7 + 613 times, incl. Hex 427 (of which 328 P, 49 E, 25 J, 25 D), Ju 6:1, SK Ch 73 (23 in ref. to ancient history, 10 in opp. to Judah); so also Vrss & var. sometimes for בֵּית יִשׂ׳, e.g. Jos 21:43 + v. Di, Ez 3:1 + v. Co; also the reverse Ez 2:3 al.; note especially עַם בּ׳ יִשְׂרָאֵל Ex 1:9; עַמִּי בּ׳ יִשְׂרָאֵל Ex 3:10; 7:4; עֲדַת בְּנֵי יִשְׂרָאֵל Ex 16:1, 2, 9, 10; 17:1 Lv 16:5; 19:2; Nu 1:2, 53; 8:9, 20; 13:26; 15:25, 26 17:6; 19:9; 25:6; 26:2; 31:12 (all P); דֹּרוֹת ב׳ יִשׂ׳ Ju 3:2; כָּל־ב׳ יִשׂ׳ וְכָל־הָעָם 20:26; ב׳ יִשׂ׳ וּבְנֵי הַלֵּוִי Ne 10:40; also (δ) בְּנֵי יְהוּדָה (lit. Gn 46:12; 26:19 1 Ch 2:3, 10; 4:1) Nu 1:26 + 18 times Nu Jos, Ju 1:8, 9, 16 (so read also v 21(×2) cf. Jos 15:53 & v. sub בנימן) 2 S 1:18 1 Ch 4:27 + 8 times Chr, Je 7:30 + 4 times Je; Ho 2:2 Jo 4:6, 8, 19 Ob 12 (not in K, of Judah or of any other tribe, except בְּנֵי לֵוִי 1 K 12:31) incl. מַטֵּה בְּנֵי יְהוּדָה Jos 15:1, 20, 21; 21:1 1 Ch 6:50; for usage with other tribes of Isr., v. the articles;—but note (ε) †בְּנֵי לֵוִי (lit. Gn 46:11 Ex 6:16 Nu 3:17 1 Ch 5:27; 6:1 cf. 23:6) Ex 32:28 Nu 3:15; 16:7, 8; 18:21 Jos 21:10 (as including sons of Aaron etc.); כָּל־בְּנֵי לֵוִי Ex 32:26; כָּל־אַחֶיךָ ב׳ ל׳ Nu 16:10; הַכֹּהֲנִים ב׳ ל׳ Dt 21:5; 31:9 cf. 1 K 12:31 & Mal 3:3; 1 Ch 23:24, 27; 24:20 Ezr 8:15 (distinguished from priests) Ne 12:23 Ez 40:46 (including בְּנֵי צָדוֹק the priests); also מַחֲנוֹת ב׳ ל׳ 1 Ch 9:18; בְּנֵי הַלֵּוִי 1 Ch 12:27 Ne 10:40; בְּנֵי הַלְּוִיִּם 1 Ch 15:15; 24:30 (cf. also לֵוִי); (ζ) בְּנֵי אַהֲרֹן (lit. Ex 28:1, 40 1 Ch 5:29; 24:1; often Aaron and his sons lit. Lv 7:34; אֶת־כֹּהֲנֵי יהוה אֶת־בְּנֵי אַהֲרֹן וְהַלְּוִיִּם 2 Ch 13:9 cf. v 10; once in sing. הַכֹּהֵן בֶּן־אַהֲרֹן Ne 10:39; v. also sub אַהֲרֹן; (η) †בְּנֵי צָדוֹק Ez 40:26, 44:15 הכהנים הלוים בני צדוק; 48:11 הכהנים הַמְֿקֻדָּשׁ מבני צדוק (𝔊 Sm Co join מ of מבני to preceding word, making pl.); (θ) בְּנֵי with names of peoples, lands, and cities, בְּנֵי כֻשִׁיִּים Am 9:7; בְּנֵי מִצְרַיִם Ez 16:26; בְּנֵי אַשּׁוּר Ez 16:28; 23:7, 9, 12, 23; ב׳ אֶרֶץ הַבְּרִית Ez 30:5 (Co del. ארץ); ב׳ בָּבֶל Ez 23:15, 17, 23; ב׳ יְרוּשָׁלִַם Jo 4:6; ב׳ צִיּוֹן Jo 2:23 La 4:2 ψ 149:2 (cf. Zc 9:13). Vid. further (ι) בְּנֵי עַמֶּ֑ךָ Lv 19:18 cf. 20:17 Nu 22:5 Ju 14:16, 17 Ez 3:11; 33:2, 12, 17, 30; 37:18 Dn 12:1; בְּנֵי פָּרִיצֵי עַמֶּךָ Dn 11:14; (κ) קֶבֶר בְּנֵי הָעָם 2 K 23:6 2 Ch 35:5, 7, 12; קִבְרֵי בְנֵי הָעָם Je 26:23; (λ) בְּנֵי־קֶדֶם Gn 29:1 Ju 7:12; 8:10 1 K 5:10 Jb 1:3 Is 11:14 Je 49:28 Ez 25:4, 10; †(μ) בְּנֵי הַמְּדִינָה Ezr 2:1 = Ne 7:6; (ν) of bulls, בְּנֵי בָשָׁן Dt 32:14 (song) cf. KloSK 1872, 254 Di. children (male and female) Gn 3:16; 21:7 Ex 21:5; 22:23; hence בְּנֵי מְנַשֶּׁה הַזְּכָרִים Jos 17:2 male children, בֵּן זָכָר Je 20:15. youth, young men (pl.) Pr 7:7 Ct 2:3. the young of animals Lv 22:28 (שׁוֹר אוֹ שֶׂה) cf. (‖ כַּנָּה; see Che trans. & crit. n.) fig. of lifeless things, בְּנֵי רֶשֶׁף sparks Jb 5:7; stars עַיִשׁ עַל־בָּנֶיהָ Jb 38:32; arrows בֶּן־קָ֑שֶׁת Jb 41:2; בְּנֵי אַשְׁפָּתוֹ) La 3:13; cf. בֶּן־גָּרְנִי i.e. corn of my threshing-floor Is 21:10. member of a guild, order or class, †בְּנֵי הַנְּבִיאִים i.e. those belonging to the prophetic order 1 K 20:35 2 K 2:3, 5, 7, 15; 4:1, 38(×2); 5:22; 6:1; 9:1 (Hoffm RSProph. 85. 388, K 15 f.; ZehnpfundBAS i.355 comp. Palm. בני שירתא men of the caravan VogNo. 4 al.; also בְּנֵי הַגּוֹלָה = exiles †Ezr 4:1; 6:19, 20; 8:35; 10:7, 16; בֶּן־הָרַקָּחִים Ne 3:8; further, in בֶּן־נֵכָר = foreigner (only P, poet., & late) †Gn 17:12, 27 Ex 12:43 Lv 22:25 Ez 44:9(×2); ב׳־הַנּ׳ †Is 56:3; בְּנֵיִ־נֵכָר †2 S 22:45, 46 = ψ 18:45, 46 Ne 9:2 Is 60:10; 61:5; 62:8 Ez 44:7 ψ 144:7, 11, בְּנֵי־הַנּ׳ Is 56:6; also בְּנֵי הַתּוֹשָׁבִים הַגָּרִים עִמָּכֶם Lv 25:45. of animals, בֶּן־בָקָר son of (the) herd, i.e. young one of the herd, בָּקָר וּבְנֵי בָקָר 1 S 14:32 cf. עֵגֶל בֶּן־בָּקָר Lv 9:2 (P); then, in general, one of the herd: fit for food Gn 18:7, 8 (J), for sacrifice Nu 15:8 (P); בן הַבּ׳ Lv 12:6 Nu 15:9 (P); especially פַּר בֶּן־בָּקָר Ex 29:1 Lv 4:3, 14; 16:3; 23:18 Nu 7:15 + 16 times Nu (all P) + 2 Ch 13:9 Ez 43:19, 23, 25; 45:18; 46:6; פָּרִים בְּנֵי בָּקָר Nu 28:11, 19, 27; 29:13, 17 (P); also בְּנִי אֲתֹנוֹ Gn 49:11 (poem, J; ‖עִירֹה); בְּנֵי־צֹאן ψ 114:4, 6; בֶּן־רְאֵמִים ψ 29:6; בְּנֵי הָרַמָּכִים Est 8:10; (הַ)יּוֹנָה בְּנֵי Lv 1:14 + 7 times Lv + Nu 6:10 cf. בֶּן־יוֹנָה Lv 12:6 (all P); בְּנֵי־נָ֑שֶׁר Pr 30:17; בְּנֵי עֹרֵב ψ 147:9. ב׳ as n. relat. followed by word of quality, characteristic, etc. especially †(α) בֶּן־(בני)חַיִל = mighty man 1 S 14:52; 18:17 2 S 2:7; 13:28; 17:10(×2) 1 K 1:52 + 7 times Ch; אֲנָשִׁים בני ח׳ Ju 18:2 2 K 2:16; אֶלָף אִישׁ מִבְּנֵי הֶחָ֑יִל Ju 21:10; †(β) בְּנֵי עַוְלָה wicked men 2 S 3:34; 7:10 1 Ch 17:9 Ho 10:9; בֶּן־ע׳ ψ 89:23 (for בני בליעל v. בליעל); †(γ) בְּנֵי מֶ֑רִי rebels Nu 17:25 (cf. בַּיִת); †(δ) בְּנֵי הַתַּעֲרֻבוֹת sons of pledges = hostages 2 K 14:14 = 2 Ch 25:24; †(ε) בְּנֵי מָוֶת i.e. those deserving of death 1 S 26:16; so בֶּן־מות 2 S 12:5; בְּנֵי תְמוּתָה appointed or exposed to death ψ 79:11; 102:21; cf. †(ζ) בִּן הַכּוֹת one worthy of smiting Dt 25:2; †(η) בְּנֵי עֹ֑נִי Pr 31:5; †(θ) בְּנֵי חֲלוֹף Pr 31:8; †(ι) בְּנֵי שָׁאוֹן Je 48:45 = tumultuous ones; so also (= שֵׁאת) בְּנֵי שֵׁת Nu 24:17 cf. RV Di al.; †(κ) בְּנֵי הַיִּצְהָר Zc 4:14 i.e. anointed ones; †(λ) בֶּן־מֶשֶׁק Gn 15:2 son of possession, i.e. heir; †(μ) הֵילֵל בֶּן־שָׁ֑חַר Is 14:12 son of dawn; †(ν) of animals בְּנֵי שָׁ֑חַץ i.e. proud beasts Jb 28:8; 41:26; (ξ) of Jonah’s gourd בִּן־לַיְלָה Jon 4:10(×2); †(ο) of a fertile hill קֶרֶן בֶּן־שֶׁמֶן Is 5:1. n. relat. of age: of men, וַיְהִי נֹחַ בֶּן־חֲמֵשׁ מֵאוֹת שָׁנָה Gn 5:32 cf. 7:6 + 71 times P; Gn 50:26 Jos 14:7, 10; 24:29 (all E); Nu 32:11 (J), Dt 31:2; also Ju 2:8 1 S 4:15 2 S 4:4; 19:33, 36 1 Ch 2:21 23:3, 24, 27; 27:3 2 Ch 24:15; 25:5; 31:16, 17 Ezr 3:8 Is 65:20(×2) Je 52:1; + 41 times SK Ch of kings at accession; note especially (incl. in above) the phrase מִבֶּן עֶשְׂרִים שָׁנָה וָמָ֑עְלָה Ex 30:14; 38:26 Nu 1:3 + 21 times Nu 1–3 + 26:2, 4; 32:11 1 Ch 23:24, 27 2 Ch 25:5 Ezr 3:8; cf. Lv 27:7 Nu 8:24; 26:62 1 Ch 23:3 & without מעלה Nu 8:25; 18:16; also מִבֶּן עשׂרים שׁנה וְעַד בֶּן־שִׁשִּׁים שָׁנָה Lv 27:3 cf. v 5, 6; מִבֶּן שׁלשׁים שׁנה ומעלה ועד בן־חמשׁים שׁנה Nu 4:3(×2) + 12 times Nu 4; מבן שׁלושׁ שׁנים וּלְמַ֫עְלָה 2 Ch 31:16 cf. v 17; & לְמִבֶּן עשׂרים שׁנה וּלְמָ֑טָה 1 Ch 27:23. of animals, (Hex all P, incl. H) בֶּן־שָׁנָה Ex 12:5; 29:38 Lv 9:3; 23:18, 19 Nu 7:17 + 28 times Nu 7, 28, 29; also Mi 6:6; בֶּן־שְׁנָתוֹ Lv 12:6; 23:12 Nu 6:12, 14 + 12 times Nu 7; also Ez 46:13. Note.—בן appears perhaps abbrev. as בּ in a few cpd. n.pr.; v. בִּדְקַר (= בֶּן־דקר?), בִּלְשָׁן, בִּמְהָל, בַּעֲלִיס, בַּעֲנָה, בִּרְשַׁע, בִּשְׁלָם (so MV after Schol. Hamâsa3 ed. Freytag; Röde libr. hist. interpr. Arabic 20, 21; but this is very uncertain, cf. Ol§227 b, p. 613).—On Lag’s explan. of אבי in some n.pr. as for אבן = בן cf. LagBN 75 & v. אבינר p. 4, etc., but this is dub.

### לִבְנֵי הָאָדָם
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: בֵּן²  

n.pr.m. a Levite 1 Ch 15:18, but del. 𝔊 Be Öt cf. v 20.

---

### לִבְנֵי הָאָדָם
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: בְּנוֹ  

1 Ch 24:26, 27 as n.pr.m. in AV RV, but render: the sons of Jaaziah his son, & the sons of Merari by Jaaziah his son, cf. VB & Be Öt.
